    Dear John

    Hillsborough - Inquests

    I attended the first day of the Hillsborough Inquests and about
    an hour of the second morning.

    Most of the first day was spent by the Coroner explaining how he
    intended to deal with the inquests, and evidence from the police,
    the blood analyst and a pathologist of a general nature.      The
    Coroner confirmed that no evidence would be called, no questions
    allowed, which related to how the deaths had occurred (other than
    the medical cause of deaths) or which involved criticism of any
    individual or organisation.

    The Coroner said that, despite what he had said at the "pre­
    inquest review" he did not intend to circulate the case summaries
    other than to the solicitors representing the families.

    I, and a number of other lawyers, had informed the Coroner that
    we intended to be present for the first day and then to attend
    only the inquests where it appeared that something might arise
    which was relevant to our clients.    I explained that, if I did
    not have access to the summaries, it would be difficult to be
    selective about which, if any, inquests to attend, and the
    Coroner decided to make the summaries available after all but
    stated that the evidence actually given may not correspond
    exactly with the contents of the summaries.

    Because of the preliminaries and the general evidence there was
    only time for one inquest on the first day so I attended the
    first three on the following morning (the Coroner intends to deal
    with eight each day).
      2

23 April 1990


In the ca s e of eac h of the    inquests which   I attended     the
following evidence was called;

1.   The pathologist who carried out the post mortem gave the
     medical cause of death.

2.   The police officer who had been assigned to the particular
      fatality gave details of the information w h i c h he had
      obtained from witnesses, so far as it was available, of the
      deceased's movements from leaving home on 15th April 1989 to
     being received at the temporary mortuary at the gymnasium at
      Hillsborough;  this was all hearsay evidence.
                                                        V

3.   The police officer who had examined photographs and video
      recordings gave details of each of the sightings of the
     deceased.

The Coroner confirmed that each of the inquests would follow this
pattern.  Each of the inquests was adjourned.

I have read the 95 case summaries and in each case where SYMAS
personnel are mentioned I have compared the contents of the
summary against our statements and the two are consistent.  In
fact the references to SYMAS personnel appear to be based upon
the statements which we took.

The solicitor representing the families of the deceased has
informed me that he has no questions to put which relate to SYMAS
involvement.

In the circumstances, unless something new arises,          I do not
intend to attend any more of the inquests.

I am sending a copy of this letter to Don Page.

Yours sincerely




A. J. CALLAGHAN

             Dear Don

             Hillsborough Disaster - Resumed Inquests

             The Coroner has still not been able to inform me whether he
             wishes to call any witnesses from SYMAS, but he has said that if
             he does wish any of your personnel to attend to give evidence we
             will be given advance details of who will be called, and the
             dates on which they are likely to be asked to give evidence.  He
             has also indicated that he will do what he can to minimise the
             inconvenience to SYMAS.

             At present I do not know precisely which points the Coroner would
             propose to go into in any detail but I imagine we will have a
             better idea of that when we know who (if anybody) he wants to
             give evidence.

             Best wishes.

             Yours sincerely




             A. J. CALLAGHAN
             Partner

 Dear Si

                                    Hillsborough Inquests

 I am actively considering the names of the people who I may wish to
 attend to give evidence at the resumed Inquests which are scheduled t
 commence on the 19th November 1990 and to continue thereafter until
 about 20th December 1990. If the hearings are not completed by then
 there will be a break for Christmas and New Year and I am planning to
 resume on about 7th January 1991.

 It is my normal practice not to issue subpoenas or summonses to
 witness who I wish to attend at Inquests as I find that most people
 are perfectly willing to come to Inquests by invitation.

You will appreciate that with Inquests of the size and complexity
associated with the Hillsborough Disaster it is very important that
the attendance of witnesses should go as smoothly as it is possible t.
arrange. I therefore would like to have your confirmation that, if I
were to request you to attend as a witness you will in fact do so
without a subpoena or summons being served upon you.

 When replying, would you please- also let me have certain other
 information which I have set out on the attached pro-forma. Please
 return this immediately to my coroner's officer (listing) in the
 envelope provided.

 If you have any questions regarding this matter please contact my
 coroner's officer (listing):

     Det. Sgt. M.J. Tredgold,  c/o West Midland Police,
     Hillsborough Inquiry, Nechells Police Station, Birmingham B7 5DA.

 His telephone number is \                                        [ Please do not phone him unless
 it is really absolutely necessary.

You will of course be contacted again with further details if your
attendance is going to be required.

                                    Hillsborough Inquiry

                                    I am writing to let you know that I am moving to this firm's
                                    Sheffield office with effect from 1st November.

                                    I have written to The Secretary to the Hillsborough Inquiry to
                                    notify Lord Justice Taylor that we have nothing to add to our

In 'Lay-Mans' terms, a description of the items of equipment specified in
your Fax dated 10th July 1989 are as follows

Ferno Ked - a device used in the removal of patients with a suspected
spinal cord injury, from a road traffic accident or works incident.

Geudal Airway - an airway inserted into the mouth to assist during
ventilation of a patient using a bag and mask, and to assist in the
maintaining of an airway in a unconscious patient.

Laerdal - A battery powered suction unit used for the removal of
secretions, ie, vomit, foreign objects from the airway.

Adaptors (feaale) - Used to join endotrachael tubes to catheter mounts.

Sphygmoaanone ter - a guage attached to an inflatable cuff used for the
measurement of a patient's blood pressure.

Vecafix - a sterile pre-packed dressing used to secure a drip in a
patient's arm.

Cannula - a small length of medical plastic into which intravenous fluids
are delivered into a patient. A cannula is inserted by using a sharp
needle which cuts into the vein. The cannula is then pushed and secured
into position in the vein,

Hartmann's Fluid - a sterile solution containing salts used in patients who
have been vomiting and are de-hydrated.

Haemaccel Fluid - a volume used for patients with severe injuries, ie,
burns, fractures.

NB:   Used extensively by the Marines in the Falklands.


The numbers following Oxygen and Entonox correspond to the check which is
carried out at the commencement of duty. This check is carried out by each
crew as they are assigned to their vehicle. Each Oxygen and Entonox set is
checked firstly for contents and then for function. The level of gases
within the set are then recorded on the form which you have a copy of.

May I add that during Lord Justice Taylor's visit to the Health and Safety
laboratories, he spent some time looking at various items of Ambulance
equipment and asked informed questions regarding the use and operation.

       Recently members of your staff were contacted by mine, with your
       permission, for the purpose of assisting us in identification evidence
       for Her Majesty's Coroner in Sheffield.

       Feedback from my officers indicates that your members of staff were
       extremely helpful to our investigation. Whilst having to view the

       diminished.

Dear Sir

Further to my telephone conversation of 10 October 1989, with your Deputy Chief
Metropolitan Ambulance Officer, Mr Hopkins, concerning the re-interviewing/showing
of video and photographic evidence to selected ambulance crews for the purpose of
trying to establish continuity between Sheffield Wednesday Football Club and
Hospitals in respect of a number of those that died in the Hillsborough Disaster on
15 April 1989. Enclosed please find a list of those Ambulance Officers that we
would like to see, together with their Ambulance Stations according to our records.

Subject to your agreement I would propose that my Inspector Layton, who is in charge
of the Video and Photographic Unit dealing with the Hillsborough Disaster, contacts
one of your staff who could act as a Liaison Officer, with a view to arranging
mutually’convenient times and dates for your ambulance officers to be seen.

Given their present level of commitments, I do not anticipate Inspector Layton and
his team will be in a position to start this aspect of our enquiry until some time
in the week commencing Monday, 23 October 1989, if this is convenient to you.

May I take this opportunity of thanking you for your co-operation and assistance in
this matter. If you have any queries please do not hesitate to contact me at
Nechells Green Police Station.

Yours faithfully
JL_______

R. Taylor
Detective Superintendent
(Hillsborough Inquiry)

Dear Mr Fozard

                 USE OF ERC CHANNEL - HILLSBOROUGH DISASTER

I write in reply to your letter of 17 July on this subject.

After all localised checks had failed to find the source of the problem,
which included extensive checks with the BBC who were covering another
sporting event in South Yorkshire, the DTI were contacted and asked to
investigate.

They reported firstly that the WYMAS site at Cookridge had been
definitely confirmed as one source of interference but that there was a
second,  much   stronger  source.  This second  source was   ultimately
identified as being your Weedley site.

The following is irrefutable.

Interference on ERC, with a blank carrier and long squelch tail following
each mobile transmission was continuous from the time of the Hillsborough
disaster until remedial action had been taken at the WYMAS site at
Cookridge and at your site at Weedley.

In view of this, I think that it             is a reasonable assumption that, as the
interference was continual until             these sites were identified, and ceased
immediately the faulty equipment              had been rectified that the two were
connected and were the sources of             the continuous problem.

I do not refute that you carry out regular checks on the functionality of
ERC equipment on a regular basis, as all Services should. I would however
be interested to know whether your checks on the Weedley site on the
weekend 6/7 May 1989 revealed any problem. One of my Officers who was in




                                                                                                   O P 6 5 /3 2 5 8 R
your area on Saturday 6 May reported to the DTI on Monday 8 May that
strong interference was    present  in the vicinity of  the Weedley
transmitter. It was not known, however, at that time, that the site was
operating on an Ambulance Service frequency nor, that  the equipment was
yours.

It might well be prudent to extend the recommendation to test dynamic two
way communications to include testing of whether base stations are on
talkthro’. This would not necessarily be apparent unless multiple
vehicles or fixed mobiles in the coverage area of each base station are
in use on the ERC frequency at the time the checks are performed.

I share your view that some form         of "Timeout Timer" should      be
incorporated in all future specifications and have indicated this to Mr
      recently.

If your Service experience is that "ERC is rarely free of interference
and is frequently used by certain services for day to day operational
control purposes", I would have thought that your own ability to
effectively control a large incident would be seriously impaired. I am,
therefore, surprised that remedial action has not taken place to cure
what is, obviously, a known situation contrary to all current guidelines
for ERC usage.

Regarding Lord Chief Justice Taylor's enquiry, I did not identify any
Service by name. I factually pointed out that this Service experienced an
interference problem which impaired our response and which had been
subsequently identified as being caused through other NHS users. I can,
therefore, state categorically that no evidence was given that could, in
any way, be misinterpreted nor be directed at any particular Service.

Yours sincerely




A Page
CHIEF METROPOLITAN AMBULANCE OFFICER

               Dear Mr Page

               Re: Pse of ERC Channel - Hillsborough Disaster

               I acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 11 July 1989- I was somewhat
               surprised at its content which I would have hoped would have been preceded by
               a telephone   call. I was even more surprised with the generalised statements
               and assumptions upon which you had reached conclusions which you had
               circulated widely in ambulance circles and to Lord Justice Taylor's Enquiry.
               My reply is more factually based and reflects the tone of your letter to me.
               I am aware that you sent a similar letter to W.Y.M.A.S.

               1.    Our standard procedures provide for only one Humberside ERC base station
                     to monitor the ERC channel on a continuous basis. This is our Caistor
                     (N Lines) base station controlled from our Scunthorpe Control. Our other
                     two base stations at Weedley and Staxton are controlled from our Anlaby
                     Control and are only 'switched in'when the need arises other than for
                     systems cheeks which are laid down in control instructions.

                     All three systems provide at the control consoles a visual indication of
                     transmitters in use and whether they are in talk-through mode or not.

               2.    On Saturday 15 April 1989 our Scunthorpe Control were monitoring ERC.
                     They were not in talk through mode. The controller received on his ERC RX
                     S.Y.M.A.S. mobile calls to their control. He did not switch his system to
                     T/T he switched in a fixed mobile and monitored base calls via his fixed
                     mobile set and mobile calls via his ERC base station receiver.

                         Our Anlaby Copntrol did not activate the ERC base stations at Weedley or
                         Staxton both stations were switched off. There was no question of the
                         stations being switched on and they were certainly not in T/T mode.

                     The controller at Anlaby was not even aware there had been a disaster
                     until 2000 hours that evening.
2.17 July 1989
Mr A Page


3.   Attached for your information, a photocopy of a control occurrence book
     entry at Anlaby showing an entry at 1317 hours on Sunday 16 April 1989
     indicating a radio check involving our major disaster vehicle and the ERC
     channel. That records a standard check procedure and, with no faults
     noted, indicated all equipment functioning correctly. The tests involved
     mobiles and mobiles in talk through mode.


4.   My investigation into the claims made in your letter have involved
     discussions with Philips depot managers at Sheffield and Leeds, and
     through them D.T.I. at Leeds. The information provided to me was as
     follows:-

     4.1 Philips, Sheffield They received an enquiry from SYMAS on 17 April
     1989 re ERC problems described as "mobiles having problems due to a second
     carrier". Philips carried out a detailed and lengthy check into all Trent
     RHA ambulance systems and no defects were found. They had no further
     involvement and believed that you pursued the matter further with D.T.I.

     4.2 Philips, Leeds and D.T.I. Leeds
     D.T.I. Leeds received an enquiry from a Mr Trueman of S.Y.M.A.S. on
     8 May 1989 ie 23 days after Hillsborough re interference on E.R.C.

     Philips were involved with D.T.I. on 11 May 1989-

     On 11 May 1989 the joint investigation found that the Humberside ERC base
     station at Weedley and the W.Y.M.A.S. ERC base station at Cookridge, Leeds
     were both in talk-through mode ie They were in talk through mode when
     checked on 11 May 1989 but this does not indicate they were in T/T mode 26
     days earlier. The Humberside Weedley station, as indicated above, was
     checked and found to be functioning correctly on 16 April 1989 and on
     subsequent standard checks in the interim period.

     The Philips engineer when checking on 11 May 1989 found, during his visit
     to the site, the Weedley transmitter to be switched on in talk through
     mode but found the control equipment showing the transmitter switched off
     and not in T/T.

     The fault has not occurred before or since and simulated testing and
     repeated switching could not reproduce the fault. I am advised by Philips
     that control of the station and talk through switching is fully
     functional. They cannot explain the malfunction on or about 11 May 1989.

I am satisfied, therefore, that:-

1.   Our Weedley ERC base station was not  switched on on 15 April 1989 nor
     was there any visible indication in our Anlaby Control of itbeing on or
     in talk through mode.

2.   Checks on 16 April 1989 and subsequently indicated all equipment
     functioning correctly at Anlaby, Weedley and Staxton.
    3
    17 July 1989
    Mr A Page


    3-   Systems and operational instructions operating in Humberside cover
         adequately the operation of the E.R.C. system, within the context of the
         systems, D.T.I. licensing and the spirit of national advice on the use of
         ERC. Other than that our systems do not include an auto disabling timer
         circuit. This will be provided in our replacement system due to be
         commissioned in December 1989.

    I find no facts to support your general assumptions and I should be grateful
    if you could correct any misleading evidence which may have been given to
    Lord Justice Taylor's Enquiry at least as it concerns the Humberside
    Ambulance Service. I have copied this letter to M/s Handy, Turnbull and Caple
    and the Yorkshire RHA.

    Finally whilst I normally avoid giving advice to other Chief Ambulance
    Officers, should your control officers experience similar difficulty in the
    future and suffer "operational anxiety and concern" they could consider
    switching control to one of your domestic channels particularly when the
    incident occurs on a Saturday afternoon. Our experience is that ERC is rarely
    free from interference and is frequently used by certain services adjacent to
    us for day to day operational control purposes due to their lack of provision
    of standby equipment. We try to act more responsibly and our operational
    procedures reflect this.

    Yours sincerely



c
    M FOZARD
    Chief Ambulance Officer

    cc   Mr   M   Handy - CAO Nottinghamshire Ambulance Service
         Mr   G   Turnbull - CAO Suffolk AmbulanceService
         Mr   L   Caple -MCAO Northumbria Ambulance Service
         Mr   T   Smith -Acting MCAO W.Y.M.A.S.
         Mr   B   Collins - Yorkshire RHA

                                                  SYMAS' Interim Recommendations


                    l.          At any match where the attendance is expected to exceed
                                25,000 and/or the past history and/or other circumstances
                                indicate that ambulance service presence is desirable (a
                                "critical match"), the holder of the safety certificate
                                should invite the appropriate ambulance authority to attend
                                with such personnel, vehicles and equipment as shall be
                                agreed between them but in any event not less than an
                                officer with one vehicle and crew, the appropriate payment
                                being made by the holder of the safety certificate.


                    2.          Each club being the holder of a safety certificate, should
                                maintain a first aid room, equipped to a basic standard. We
                                attach hereto a suggested minimum*


                    3.          The Football League, Football Association and the relevant
                                licensing authority should require clubs holding safety
                                certificates and intending to stage a critical match to
                                arrange that the doctor in attendance:

                                a.    is trained to advanced first aid standard (ie NHS TA
                                      Paramedic) with annual recertification;

                                b.    is available to treat injured spectators and to attend
                                      the first aid room, when called upon to do so.


                    4,          For all designated grounds there should be a joint and
                                common emergency plan drawn up by the relevant po li c e
                                authority, fire service, ambulance service and the holder of
                                the safety certificate, The emergency plan should detail:

                                a.    The r e l e v a n t   function          of        each         se r v i c e   during        an
                                      emergency.

                                b.   The liaison officer from each service, preferably by
                                     name, t hei r r e s p e c t i v e s t a t i o n a n d p a r t i c u l a r
                                     responsibility.

                                c.   The agreed rendezvous points for each service and the
                                     (named) police officer who is responsible for collating
                                     and i ssui ng i n f o r m a t i o n to, and coordinating the
                                     response of, the relevant services.

                                d.   Emergency access and egress.

                                e.   Casualty clearing point.


                                                                   1

                                                                                                                        P. 0 2
       FROM         D   L   B   SOLICITORS
                                                                    7.   4 . 1 9 8 9   1 1 : 0 4
                                                                                                             P .   3




                                f,    preferred routes to and from hospitals, to be kept clear
                                      by the police during an emergency.


                    5.          Before each critical match the liaison officers from the
                                police and ambulance service should make contact and arrange
                                to be in direct contact with each other during the match.
                                The police liaison officer should be in direct contact with
                                police control.


                    6.          The relevant ambulance service should be asked to advise the
                                issuing authority for each s a f e t y c e r t i f i c a t e on the
                                question of:

                                a*    Access and egress for ambulances.

                                b.    Medical equipment.

                                c.    A t t e n d a n c e of a m b u l a n ce personnel            and vehicles        at
                                      critical matches.

                                               Criticisms of SYMAS


             A.        Criticisms of SYMAS appeared in quotations contained in an
                       article which appeared in The Times and The Independent of
                       17 April 1989* They also appeared in other newspapers. The
                       remarks were made by two doctors who attended Hillsborough
                       as Liverpool supporters on 15 April 1989* During the course
                       of the evidence it b e c a m e c lear that the first rive
                       criticisms by Doctor A related to events in the area behind
                       the Leppings Lane stand and terrace (day 25 pl2lD).

                       DOCTOR A
                        1,    "At 3.30 pm there were no medical personnel apart from
                             one or two St John's ambulancemen.   There was no medical
                             equipment and there was only one ambulance,"

                             FACTS:
                             a.    Ambulances arriving at Leppings Lane (see video RJH2)

                                   1513     S407 arrives (leaves 1523)
                                   1516     S203 arrives (leaves 1528)
                                            (by 1530            from this ambulance is on
                                            the pitch - video MAPI)
                                   1521     S208 arrives (leaves 1532)
                                   1535     R608 arrives
                                   This doctor cannot have arrived                         in the concourse
                                   until about 1535.
                             b.    About 40 casualties are taken from the Leppings Lane
                                   end by SYMAS, of which 5 were removed by 1532.   All
                                   casualties had been removed from that end of the
                                   ground by 1606 (SYMAS recording track 19 page 19).
                                   Therefore about 35 casualties, all of whom survived
                                   were conveyed by SYMAS vehicles between 1532 and
                                   1606.

                       2,    "Lack of triage"

                             FACTS:

                             a.    SYMAS personnel operated triage:
                                   _   see               statement, paragraph 7
                                   -   see Mr Yoxall's evidence - day 23 page 12oF
                                   -   see               statement - paragraphs IS to 18
                                   -   see              statement  - paragraphs 16 and 21

                                     - see                            Ievidence - day 17 page 159E-H

                        3.     "A full casualty team with resuscitation equipment did
                               not arrive from the hospital until about 4.15 pm."

                               FACTS:

                               a.    A full casualty team arrived at the Penistone Road
                                     end of the ground.             I's statement paragraph
                                     12 shows that the medical team left Northern General
                                     Hospital at 1544; it would therefore have been at the
                                     ground by 1550 at the very latest.

                               b.    a casualty team did not attend at Leppings Lane and
                                     all casualties had been removed from that end of the
                                     ground by 1606 (see above).

                               c.    A c a s u a l t y team a r r i v e d from Barnsley District
                                      General Hospital but was not required.

                               d.    The implication that only the casualty teams would
                                      have resuscitation equipment is wrong.                            All SYMAS
                                      f r o n t l i n e a m b u l a n c e s c a r r y p o r t a b l e and   fixed
                                      resuscitation equipment which was used atLeppings
                                      Lane.          Fire s e r v i c e also carried resuscitation
                                      equipment,

                               a.        Oxygen       - Wrong, see above,

                               b.        No airways - Wrong:

                                                              were Inserted.

                                                              lack of intubation equipment, such
                                                              equipment is carried by paramedics
                                                              for use by them as they are trained
                                                              to use it. Even if there had been a
                                                              supply of such equipment available
                                                              for use by o t h e r s t h e r e is no
                                                              evidence that t here was anyone
                                                              present (apart from paramedics) who
                                                              could safely have used It. (This

                                    4 minutes and therefore it is unlikely that the

                        DOCTOR P
                        6.     "Complains about the lack of defibrillators"

                               FACTSl
                               a.   We b e l i e v e that it is accepted that the use of
                                    defibrillators on the pitch in the circumstances of
                                    this t r a g e d y wo u l d have been professionally
                                    irresponsible (day 24 page 1G3F).

                               b.   In case anybody has any doubts about it we list the
                                    difficulties!

                                        i.   If wrongly used it can kill:

                                             - the patient
                                             - the operator
                                             - any third party coming into contact with the
                                               patient.
                                     ii,     B e f o r e b eing us e d national guidelines for
                                             ambulancemen lay down that an ECG r e a ding
                                             should be obtained from the machine.            In order
                                             for this to be accurate the patient and the
                                             defibrillator must be completely isolated from
                                             any movement and the surrounding environment
                                             must be s u f f i c i e n t l y st a b l e to permit an
                                             accurate assessment of the ECG recorded -

                                    lil,     in the circumstances of this tragedy when the
                                             injured were hypoxic (lack of oxygen), the
                                             usual heart pattern is asystole (continuous
                                             line) and not ventricular fibrillation.                    The
                                             p u r p o s e of a d e f i b r i l l a t o r is to jolt a
                                             fibrillating heart rhythm back into a normal
                                             pattern,       In extreme circumstances the use of a
                                             defibrillator on a patient in asystole might
                                             produce ventricular fibrillation which can then
                                             be further treated with a defibrillator to
                                             p r o d u c e a normal pattern.              The s t a n d a r d
                                             treatment for asystole is CPR,               Successful CPR
                                             would normally produce a regular heart pattern
                                             rather than ventricular fibrillation.

                                     iv.     However given the length of time during which
                                             most of the patients had been d e p r i v e d of
                                             oxygen even the extreme use of a defibrillator

                                    (see Dr P's statement to the police, page 7 rtmy
                                    thoughts that in view of the length of time he
                                    was pulseless it would probably be kinder if he
                                    died.   I am sure he must have been severely
                                    brain damaged by that time" and at the bottom

          B,          Ambulance Equipment
                      A list of equipment carried by all SYMAS frontline ambulances
                      is appended hereto.   This equipment is Intended for use by
                      SYMAS personnel   and not by third parties.    Much    of the
                      equipment has to be kept with the ambulance    Cor use on the
                      journey to hospital.  The primary purpose of the ambulance is
                      to give immediate treatment to casualties and convey them to
                      hospital. On 15 April 1989 many SYMAS ambulancemen rendered
                      first aid (CPR and resuscitation) as well as conveying 172
                      injured to hospital.   Any criticism of lack of equipment cn
                      SYMAR vehicles is ill-informed.

F R OM   D   L   B   SOLICITORS                          ?.   4. 1989   1 1 : 13            P.   4




                                  4 minutes and therefore it is unlikely that the
                                  presence or otherwise of such equipment made any
                                  practical difference.

                      DOCTOR P

                      6.   "Complains about the lack of defibrillators"

                           FACTS;

                           a.     We b e l i e v e that it is accepted that the use of
                                  defibrillators on the pitch in the circumstances of
                                  this t r a g e d y w o u l d h a v e been professionally
                                  irresponsible (day 24 page 103F).

                           b.     In case anybody has any doubts about it we list the
                                  difficulties;

                                    i.   If wrongly used it can kill;

                                         - the patient
                                         - the operator
                                         - any third party coming into contact with the
                                           patient.

                                   ii.   B e f o r e b e i n g used national guidelines for
                                         ambulancemen lay down that an ECG r e a d i n g
                                         should be obtained from the machine.              In order
                                         for this to be accurate the patient and the
                                         defibrillator must be completely isolated from
                                         any movement and the surrounding environment
                                         mu s t be s u f f i c i e n t l y st a b l e to permit an
                                         accurate assessment of the ECG recorded.

                                  iii.   In the circumstances of this tragedy when the
                                         injured were hypoxic (lack of oxygen), the
                                         usual heart pattern is asystole (continuous
                                         line) and not ventricular fibrillation.                 The
                                         p u r p o s e of a d e f i b r i l l a t o r is to jolt a
                                         fibrillating heart rhythm back into a normal
                                         pattern,      in extreme circumstances the use of a
                                         defibrillator on a patient in asystole might
                                         produce ventricular fibrillation which can then
                                         be further treated with a defibrillator to
                                         p r o d u c e a normal pattern.              The s t andard
                                         treatment for asystole is CPR.               Successful CPR
                                         would normally produce a regular heart pattern
                                         rather than ventricular fibrillation.

                                  iv.    However given the length of time during which
                                         most of the patients had been d e p r i v e d of
                                         oxygen even the extreme use of a defibrillator

                                                          4
F R OM   D   L   B   SOLICITORS                 ?.       4.1989   11!14     P.   5




                                   would not have made any practical difference
                                   (see Dr p *b statement to the police, page 7 "my
                                   thoughts that in view of the length of time he
                                   was pulseless it would probably be kinder if he
                                   died.   i am sure he must have been severely
                                   brain damaged by that time" and at the bottom
                                   of the page "I looked desperately for somebody
                                   not dead but seriously ill who needed help*    I
                                   could not find anybody in that category in the
                                   goal area.   They were either all dead or okay
                                   with nothing in between."

             B.      Ambulance Equipment

                     A list of equipment carried by all SYMAS frontline ambulances
                     is appended hereto.   This equipment is intended for use by
                     SYMAS personnel and not by third parties.        Much of the
                     equipment has to be kept with the ambulance for use on the
                     journey to hospital. The primary purpose of the ambulance is
                     to give immediate treatment to casualties and convey them to
                     hospital. On 15 April 1989 many SYMAS ambulancemen rendered
                     first aid (CPR and resuscitation) as well as conveying 172
                     injured to hospital.   Any criticism of lack of equipment on
                     SYMAS vehicles is ill-informed.

HILLSBOROUGH STADIUM DISASTER - INTERIM REPORT
OF THE RT HON LORD JUSTICE TAYLOR


Report of the Regional General Manager


1.     As Members will be aware, the Interim Report of the Inquiry
       set up by the Home Secretary into the disaster at the
       Hillsborough Football Stadium on 15th April 1989 was published
       on Friday, 4th August 1989.


2.     Attached for the information of Members are relevant extracts
       from the Report relating to the South Yorkshire Metropolitan
        Ambulance Service.   Also enclosed is a copy of a short
        press statement issued after publication.


3.     Mr A Page, Chief Metropolitan Ambulance Officer, will be in
        attendance at the meeting to answer any queries and to report
        on the up-to-date situation.

    No Fault by the Emergency Services
       296. I say at th e o u tse t o f th is c h a p te r th a t no valid criticism can be m a d e o f th e response by the St Jo h n
    A m b u la n ce Brigade, by SYM AS o r by the fire brigade o n 15 April, in deed , no represented party v en tu red any
    criticism o f th em . T he only attac k o n SYM AS cam e from tw o Liverpool doctors. O n e claim ed th a t am b ulan ces
    d id n o t arriv e swiftly o r w ith sufficient e q u ip m e n t a n d th a t there was a lack o f triage. He was proved to be
    w rong in all th ree respects. U n fo rtu n a tely he h ad seen fit to go on television o n 15 April w hen he said m ore
    lives could have been saved if staff a n d e q u ip m e n t h ad arriv ed earlier. A p art from being proved w rong in fact
    as to th e tim e s o f arrival o f am b u lan ces, he co nceded in evidence th a t it was no t possible to say w h ether lives
    could have been saved. H is co m m en ts o n television were irresponsible. T h e o th e r d o cto r co m plained o f the
    absence o f defibrillators. I am satisfied o n overw helm ing evidence th a t to a tte m p t to use a d efib rillator o n the
    p itch w ith people m illing ab o u t w ould have been highly dan g ero u s owing to th e risk o f injury from the electric
    charge.

      297. I find th a t all three o f th e em ergency services n a m e d above resp on d ed pro m p tly w hen alerted, th a t
    they b rou g ht a p p ro p ria te e q u ip m e n t a n d th a t th e ir personnel o p era ted efficiently.

        298. It w ould be u n rea so n ab le to expect, at any sp o rts sta d iu m , m edical facilities capable o f dealing with a
    m a jo r disa ste r such as occurred. To have in ad v a n ce at th e ground, oxygen, resuscitators, stretchers, o th e r
    e q u ip m e n t a n d m edical staff sufficient to deal w ith over 100 casualties is not practicable.

       299. W h a t is req u ired is a basic level o f provision for first aid, for professional m edical a tten tio n a n d for
    a m b u la n c e a tte n d a n c e , together w ith a system o f co -o rd in atio n w ith the em ergency services w hich will bring
    th e m to th e scene swiftly in w hatever n u m b e rs are required. W h a t will a m o u n t to an a p p ro p riate basic
    p ro v isio n for th e fu tu re f g th e e q u ip m e n t in a first aid room , requires expert ev alu atio n a n d advice.

    to a tte n d at th e first aid ro o m if re q u ire d ” , as laid dow n in th e Safety Certificate.

     th a t SY M A S a n d th e fire brigade should have been called earlier th a n they were. However, in view o f th e natu re
     a n d ex ten t o f th e cru sh in g , th e tim e w hen police rescue began a n d the pathetically short p erio d for w hich those
     u n ab le to b rea th e co u ld survive, it is im p ro b a b le th a t q u ick er recourse to th e em ergency services w ould have
     saved m o re lives.

     o f Ihe grou n d to allow a n a m b u la n c e o n to th e p itch a n d th a t at th e to p o t th e ra m p leading to th e p itch the

     was fru stra ted by th e ^ r e s e n c e o f vehicles ju s t inside th e ground. In th e event, n on e o f these m a tte rs affected
     o p era tio n s. T h e h o a x i n g was quickly knock ed dow n. T h e difficult access to th e ra m p was n egotiated an d

Part IV - Interim Recommendations

Safety- Certificates
       13. There should be an immediate review of each Safety Certificate by the responsible local authority,
    which should consult the club in respect of which the Certificate is issued, the police, the fire service, the
    ambulance service and the building authority. Such a review should include an inspection of the stadium. Its
    object should be to ensure that the operative conditions of the Certificate are complied with and to add or
    substitute any condition shown to be necessary as a matter of urgency following the findings and
    recommendations in this report.

      14. Any local authority w ithin whose area there exists a sports stadium designated under the 1975 Act for
    which no Safety Certificate has yet been issued should proceed forthwith to remedy the situation.

      15. Every Safety Certificate should be reviewed by the local authority at least once annually and each
    Certificate should require to be renewed annually.


       16. Each local authority should review its arrangements for issuing, monitoring, enforcing, reviewing,
    amending and renewing Safety Certificates. Such review should require that there exists or is provided an
    accountable administrative structure whereby the functions of the local authority are regularly and
    effectively supervised by senior officers and elected members and decisions are properly taken in accordance
    with the lodnauthority's rules.

       17. To assist the local authority in exercising its functions, it should set up an Advisory Group consisting
    of appropriate members of its own staff, representatives of the police, of the fire and ambulance services, of
    the building authority, of the club and of a recognised supporters' club. The Advisory Group's terms of
    reference should encompass all matters concerned with crowd safety and should require regular visits to the
    ground and attendance at matches. The Advisory Group should have a chairman from the local authority,
    and an effective procedure. Its resolutions should be recorded and it should be required to produce regular
    written reports for consideration by the local authority.



Co-ordination of Emergency Services
★      35. The police, fire and ambulance services should maintain through senior nominated officers regular
    liaison concerning crowd safety at each stadium.

★     36. Before each match at a designated stadium, the police should ensure that the fire service and
    ambulance service are given full details about the event, including its venue, its timing, the number of
    spectators expected, their likely routes of entry and exit, and any anticipated or potential difficulties
    concerning the control or movement of the crowd. Such details should be readily available in the control
    rooms of each of the emergency services.

★     37. Contingency plans for the arrival at each designated stadium of emergency vehicles from all three
    services should be reviewed. They should include routes of access, rendezvous points, and accessibility
    within the ground itself.

★     38. Police officers posted at the entrances to the ground should be briefed as to the contingency plans for
    the arrival of emergency services and should be informed when such services are called as to where and why
    they are required.


First Aid, M edical Facilities and Ambulances
★     39. There should be at each stadium at each match at least one trained first aider per 1,000 spectators. The
    club should have the responsibility for securing such attendance.

      40. There should be at each stadium one or more first aid rooms. The number o f such rooms and the
    equipment to be maintained within them should be specified by the local authority after taking professional
    medical advice and should be made a requirement of any Safety Certificate.

★     41. The club should eiat>iu> a nieuical practitioner to be present at each match and available to deal with
    any medical exigency at the ground. H e should be trained and competent in advanced first aid. H e should be
    present at the ground at least an hour before kick-off and should remain until half an hour after the end o f the
    match. H is whereabouts should be known to those in the police control room and he should be immediately
    contactable.
                 f c-

★     42. At least one fully equipped ambulance from the appropriate ambulance authority should be in
    attendance at all matches with an expected crowd of 5,000 or more.

      43. The number of ambulances to be in attendance for matches where larger crowds are expected should
    be specified by the local authority after consultation with the ambulance service and should be made a
    requirement o f the Safety Certificate.

HILLSBOROUGH INQUIRY REACTION
SOUTH YORKSHIRE METROPOLITAN AMBULANCE SERVICE


   Mr. Brian Edwards, General Manager of Trent Regional Health
Authority, commented today (Friday, August 4) on the interim
report of the Hillsborough Inquiry.
    "We have always had every confidence in the professionalism
and dedication of the personnel of the South Yorkshire
Metropolitan Ambulance Service, which is one of the finest in
the country.
    "It is very nice to know that Lord Justice Tayler has reached
the same conclusion".

23rd August 1989.


PRESS RELEASE - FOR IMMEDIATE RELEASE




The publication of the interim report by the Right Honourable Lord Justice
Taylor into the Hillsborough disaster has reinforced the recommendations
made by South Yorkshire Metropolitan Ambulance Service "that professional
ambulance officers and crews should be on standby at league football and
large sporting events".


Mr. A. Page, Chief Metropolitan Ambulance Officer, took immediate action
following the Bradford Fire disaster and prepared contingency plans in the
event of a large scale incident at a local football match, consequently, on
the day of the Hillsborough tragedy SYMAS officers were in a position to
respond immediately.


Throughout the judicial enquiry, senior and operational officers gave oral
and written evidence to Lord Justice Taylor.      A comprehensive and detailed
list of recommendations from SYMAS have been included in the report and
were welcomed and supported by Mr. Page.


The general public can now be assured that resulting from the efforts of
the South Yorkshire Metropolitan Ambulance Service, which is now recognised
as the leading authority on the planning and response to large scale
incidents, the provision of emergency care will be of the highest order.




E nds.




If you require any further information, please contact                            ,
Public Relations Officer, SYMAS, ext 257.

              SOUTH YORKSHIRE METROPOLITAN AMBULANCE SERVICE

                         DEPARTMENTAL MEMORANDUM



Refi    AP/                                 Date:   23rd August 1989

From:   Mr. A. Page,                       To:      All members of staff




HILLSBOROUGH DISASTER - INTERIM REPORT


I circulate for your interest the relevant chapter of the report
associated to SYMAS. You will see that Lord Justice Taylor has confirmed
that SYMAS conducted themselves efficiently and no blame or fault can be
associated to this service.

I also enclose chapter 24 of the interim report which gives details of
the recommendations made by Lord Justice Taylor.

As a service we should be extremely proud and pleased to see that within
the recommendations, the Ambulance Service has been given far more
responsibility and input in terms of service provision and liaison
committees. It is only because of the actions of the South Yorkshire
Metropolitan Ambulance Service on the day and the effective way in which
we made our representation to the enquiry, that these recommendations
have materialised.

SYMAS has many things to be proud of and we have once again been
extremely successful in ensuring that the Ambulance Service nationally,
as well as locally is taking up a position of importance in the future.

I would like to once again thank and congratulate all of my staff for
their efforts on the day and since, in order to ensure that the service
has returned as quickly as possible to normal.
          INQUIRY BY
THE RT HON LORD JUSTICE TAYLOR




       INTERIM RECOMMENDATIONS
                                 CHAPTER 24-THE RECOMMENDATIONS
o m . m H ™ recommendations which follow are the m inimum necessary to ensure that existing football
fnformaHon i ha Present be safely used based upon the evidence which I have heard at Sheffield and other
after his^nquiry atBradford     com P*ement the recommendations which Mr Justice Popplewell set out


   3 ! 6 . T ^ ose which are asterisked should be carried out before the 1989/90 season starts. The rest should be
started forthwith and com pleted as soon as possible.

M axim um C apacities for Terraces
★                  * view ing terrace is divided into pens or areas w hich are self-contained, the S afety C ertificate
    sh ould sp ecify the m axim um num ber o f spectators to be adm itted to each such pen or area.

    A pen or area is to be deem ed “ self-contained" notw ithstanding that it has a g a te or g ates affording a ccess to
    another pen or area and w hether such gate or g ates be open or shut.

★     2; E.a c^* fi8Mre for m axim um capacity sh ould be a sse ssed in accordance with C hapter 16 o f the Green
    G uide (“th e G reen G uide figure” ) subject to the follow ing q ualifications:-

    (a) in arriving at “ the G reen G uide figure”, proper and realistic allow ance m ust be m ade for a ll factors
        w hich sh ould reduce the p erm issible d en sity in accordance w ith th e range o f m axim um figures specified
        in paragraphs 221 and 2 2 2 o f the G reen Guide;

    (b) after a sse ssin g “th e G reen G uide figure”, a deduction o f 15% should be m ade to improve the safety
        m argin.

★     3 . A rrangem ents sh ould be m ad e:-

    (a) to lim it the number o f spectators entering each self-contained pen or area to the m avim nm ^ p a ^ ify
        figure a sse sse d in accordance w ith R ecom m endation 2 above eith er electronically, m echanically, by a
        tick etin g arrangem ent, by counting or otherw ise, and

    (b) to clo se o ff further a ccess to such pen or area w hen its m axim um capacity is about to be reached.


F illing and M onitoring Terraces
★     4. T h ere sh ould be a w ritten agreem ent between the club and the p olice settin g out their respective
    resp on sib ilities for crowd safety and control and in particular for the fillin g o f each self-contained pen or
    other terraced area and the m onitoring o f spectators in each such pen or area to avoid overcrowding. Any
    variation o f th e agreem ent in respect o f an individual m atch should be agreed in w riting in advance.

★     5. A t each m atch, there sh ould be on the perim eter track, for each self-contained pen or other terraced
    area, a stew ard ( iilh e club is m onitoring that area) or a police officer (if the police are m onitoring it) w hose
    so le duty is to ch eck crowd con d itions in that area for p ossib le overcrowding or d istress throughout the
    period the area is occupied by spectators. W hoever is so appointed should be in addition to any other steward
    or police presence. H e should have ready a ccess to the police officer authorised under Recom m endation 9
    below, and by radio to the control room.


Fences and G ates
       6 . A ll police officers and stew ards w ith duties in relation to the terraced areas and especially those w ith
    d uties under R ecom m endation 5 above, should be fully briefed and trained with regard to the recognition o f
    crowd d en sities, to the recognition o f sig n s o f d istress and to crowd dynam ics. Training should include
    dem onstrations a t the ground and photographs, d esign ed to enable stew ards and officers to recognise
    different crowd d en sities.

*     7. A ll g ates in radial or perim eter fences o f pens or other self-contained areas should be painted in a
    different colour from the rest o f the fence and m arked “ Em ergency E xit” .



                                                           57
*    8. W here there are perimeter fences, all gates to the pitch should be kept fully open during the period
    when spectators are on the terrace.

*     9. There should be in respect of each gate in a perimeter fence (or group of gates if they are close together)
    a Police officer authorised to decide whether or not to allow spectators through a gate to relieve overcrowding.
    I he officer so authorised in respect of each gate should be identified for each match and known to be so by all
    stewards and police officers in that vicinity. He should be able to make radio contact with the control room
    either him self or via an officer close at hand.

*     10. Suitable and sufficient cutting equipment should be provided by the club at each ground where there
    are perim eter fences to permit the immediate removal of enough fencing to release numbers of spectators if
    necessary. Agreement should be reached as to whether the equipment should be used by police, the fire
    brigade or stewards. W hoever is to use it should be trained to do so. W hether to use it should be a decision of a
    nominated senior police officer at the ground.


Crush Barriers
*      11. All crush barriers should be visually inspected immediately for signs of corrosion. Any barrier found
    to be affected by a significant degree of corrosion should be repaired or replaced.

*      12. The layout of barriers in each pen or terraced area should be reviewed immediately to ensure th at it
    complies with the criteria contained in C hapter 9 of the Green Guide. If it does not, the assessm ent of the
    maximum capacity figure for that pen or terraced area, in accordance with Recommendation 2 above, should
    reflect the fact.


Safety C ertificates
      13. There should be an immediate review of each Safety Certificate by the responsible local authority,
    which should consult the club in respect of which the Certificate is issued, the police, the fire service, the
    ambulance service and the building authority. Such a review should include an inspection of the stadium. Its
    object should be to ensure that the operative conditions of the Certificate are complied with and to add or
    substitute any condition shown to be necessary as a m atter of urgency following the findings and
    recommendations in this report.

      14. Any local authority within whose area there exists a sports stadium designated under the 1975 Act for
    which no Safety Certificate has yet been issued should proceed forthwith to remedy the situation.

      15. Every Safety Certificate should be reviewed by the local authority at least once annually and each
    Certificate should require to be renewed annually.


       16. Each local authority should review its arrangements for issuing, monitoring, enforcing, reviewing,
    amending and renewing Safety Certificates. Such review should require that there exists or is provided an
    accountable administrative structure whereby the functions of the local authority are regularly and
    effectively sipervised by senior officers and elected members and decisions are properly taken in accordance
    with the locaTauthority's rules.

       17. To assist the local authority in exercising its functions, it should set up an Advisory Group consisting
    of appropriate members of its own staff, representatives of the police, of the fire and ambulance services, of
    the building authority, of the club and of a recognised supporters' club. The Advisory Group's terms of
    reference should encompass all m atters concerned with crowd safety and should require regular visits to the
    ground and attendance at matches. The Advisory Group should have a chairm an from the local authority,
    and an effective procedure. Its resolutions should be recorded and it should be required to produce regular
    written reports for consideration by the local authority .

*      18. Each turnstile should be inspected and its potential rate of flow measured. Thereafter, regular
    inspections should be made to ensure that each turnstile remains capable of admitting spectators at the rate
    anticipated.

    ★    .    The 9 hief Constable of each police force in whose area there is one designated stadium or more

        any foreseeable pattern in the arrival of spectators at a match and in their departure. It should provide for
        sufficient reserves to enable rapid deployment of officers to be made at any point inside or outside the ground.

    ★    29. T he option to postpone kick-off should be in the discretion of the officer in command at the ground.
        Crowd safety should be the paramount consideration in deciding whether to exercise it.

        monitoring outside and inside the ground and the record of any electronic or mechanical counting of numbers
        at turnstiles or of numbers admitted to any area of the ground. Officers in the control room should be skilled
        in the interpretation and use of these data.

        received, evaluated and answered. The radio system should be such as to give operators in the control room

        used, where necessary, to prevent overcrowding of the airwaves. Consideration should be given to
        sound-proofing the control room against excessive crowd noise.

First Aid, Medical Facilities and Ambulances
★      39, T here should be at each stadium at each match at least one trained first aider per 1,000 spectators. The
    club should have the responsibility for securing such attendance.

      40. T here should be at each stadium one or more first aid rooms. The number of such rooms and the
    equipment to be maintained within them should be specified by the local authority after taking professional
    medical advice and should be made a requirement of any Safety Certificate.

★     41. The club should employ a medical practitioner to be present at each match and available to deal with
    any medical exigency at the ground. H e should be trained and competent in advanced first aid. He should be
    present at the ground at least an hour before kick-off and should remain until half an hour after the end of the
    match. H is whereabouts should be known to those in the police control room and he should be immediately
    contactable.^-

★      42. At least one fully equipped ambulance from the appropriate ambulance authority should be in
    attendance at all matches with an expected crowd of 5,000 or more.

      43. The number of ambulances to be in attendance for matches where larger crowds are expected should
    be specified by the local authority after consultation with the ambulance service and should be made a
    requirement of the Safety Certificate.




                                                             60
                                                 CHAPTER 21

                                FIRST AID AND EMERGENCY SERVICES

No Fault by the Emergency Services
   296. I say at the outset o f this chapter that no valid criticism can be m ade o f the response by the St John
Ambulance Brigade, by SYMAS or by the fire brigade on 15 April. Indeed, no represented party ventured any
criticism o f them . The only attack on SYMAS came from two Liverpool doctors. O neclaim ed that ambulances
did not arrive swiftly or with sufficient equipm ent and that there was a lack o f triage. He was proved to be
wrong in all three respects. U nfortunately he had seen fit to go on television on 15 April when he said more
lives could have been saved if staff and equipm ent had arrived earlier. Apart from being proved wrong in fact
as to the times o f arrival o f ambulances, he conceded in evidence that it was not possible to say whether lives
could have been saved. His com m ents on television were irresponsible. The other doctor complained o f the
absence o f defibrillators. I am satisfied on overwhelming evidence that to attem pt to use a defibrillator on the
pitch with people milling about would have been highly dangerous owing to the risk o f injury from the electric
charge.

  297. I find that all three o f the emergency services nam ed above responded prom ptly when alerted, that
they brought appropriate equipm ent and that their personnel operated efficiently.

  298. It would be unreasonable to expect, at any sports stadium , medical facilities capable o f dealing with a
m ajor disaster such as occurred. To have in advance at the ground, oxygen, resuscitators, stretchers, other
equipm ent and medical staff sufficient to deal with over 100 casualties is not practicable.

  299. W hat is required is a basic level o f provision for first aid, for professional medical attention and for
am bulance attendance, together with a system o f co-ordination with the emergency services which will bring
them to the scene swiftly in whatever num bers are required. W hat will am ount to an appropriate basic
provision for the future eg the equipm ent in a first aid room, requires expert evaluation and advice.

   300. On the day, there was no clear understanding between the Club and D r Purcell as to his role. He
believed he was prim arily there to attend to the players. The Club regarded him also as the “physician available
to attend at the first aid room if required”, as laid down in the Safety Certificate.

  301. There was insufficiently close co-operation between the police and the emergency services. It is d e a r
that SYMAS and the fire brigade should have been called earlier than they were. However, in view o f the nature
and extent o f the crushing, the tim e when police rescue began and the pathetically short period for which those
unable to breathe could survive, it is im probable that quicker recourse to the emergency services would have
saved m ore lives.

   302. Finally, there was evidence that an advertising board had to be knocked down at the north-east corner
o f the ground to allow an am bulance onto the pitch and that at the top o f the ram p leading to the pitch the
access for am bulances was inadequate. There was also evidence that the pre-match arrangement for
am bulances to use the two gates from Penistone Road at the north-east end o f the ground as in and out routes
was frustrated by theipresence o f vehicles just inside the ground. In the event, none o f these m atters affected
operations. The hoarding was quickly knocked down. The difficult access to the ram p was negotiated and
am bulances used one entrance satisfactorily by backing out when loaded.

PRESS RELEASE - FOR IMMEDIATE RELEASE


HILLSBOROUGH ENQUIRY - VIGTORY FOR AMBULANCE SERVIGE

Don Page, Chief Metropolitan Ambulance Officer for the South Yorkshire
Metropolitan Ambulance Service who appeared before Lord Justice Taylor to
give evidence to the public enquiry concerning the Hillsborough Football
Stadium Disaster has now voiced publicly his feelings for the first time
about the initial recommendations made       by Lord Justice Taylor.


Mr. Page has said that he is extremely       pleased that his service and staff
have been publicly cleared of any accusations over poor response.               This
comment was particularly important because of what the enquiry identified
as irresponsible and factually incorrect comments made by one Liverpool
Doctor on television and to the media.


During the Judicial hearing the South Yorkshire Metropolitan Ambulance
Service gave evidence concerning their role during the disaster.               It came
to light that Mr, Page had ordered his       service to provide specialambulance
cover for the Hillsborough Ground for the past         four years as a
precautionary measure.       This follows the Bradford Football Club fire,


Mr, Page's actions in doing this showed foresight and was in fact one of
the major reasons for the successful ambulance service operation on the
15th April this year.     Not only were ambulances and officers in the ground
and surrounding area, there was also a well prepared plan of action in the
Ambulance Control to cater specifically for the stadium disaster.




                                                                                           C P65/32586
17th August 1989,




These actions allowed the South Yorkshire Ambulance Service to have 42
ambulances at Hillsborough within a short period of time.     Thirty one of
those ambulances belonging to South Yorkshire,     Some 172 casualties were
removed to hospital within 70 minutes and the prompt action of the South
Yorkshire Ambulance Service undoubtedly reassured the injured supporters
and reduced suffering.


Of the recommendations contained in the report, Mr, Page is cautiously
optimistic that other Ambulance Services and Football Clubs will develop
the relationship which is evident within South Yorkshire, which has five
Football Clubs to provide ambulance services to.


The recommendations made by Lord Justice Taylor were submitted largely by
Mr, Page and have been accepted as a victory for the Ambulance Services of
the United Kingdom.


For the first time ever, ambulance services have been given the opportunity
to be members of Safety Advisors Groups and provide advice on safety
matters at Sports Grounds and other Stadiums licensed by Local Authorities,
Professional Ambulance Service cover should be provided to clubs where
5,000 or more people are in attendance.


Although very proud of the efforts of his staff and pleased that because of
their actions and the foresight within his service, the disaster was
efficiently handled, Mr. Page warns that he would like to see the
recommendations made law because he fears that after the initial wave of
involvement, organisations may decide to withdraw their interest, leaving
the matter of crowd safety in jeopardy once again.
17th August 1989,




Mr, Page said "any Ambulance Service or sporting organisation which decides
to be lax about their approach to these recommendations are putting the
lives of the general public at risk.   That would be the biggest insult to
the memory of the unfortunate 95 people who died so tragically.   At least
it can be said that their deaths have assisted in opening closed minds to
the professional role of the Ambulance Service in the United Kingdom and
increasing awareness for the needs of safety of spectators at sporting
events.


He admits that he and every one of his staff have suffered greatly from the
tragedy they faced.   The South Yorkshire Ambulance Service will never
forget the feeling of devastation that day and will always have sympathy
for the victims and their families.
