     REVIEW OF ARRANGEMENTS


FOR DEALING WITH CIVIL EMERGENCIES


            IN THE UK




          HOME OFFICE


           June 1989
REVIEW of ARRANGEMENTS FOR DEALING WITH CIVIL KMER6ENCIES IN
SEE T O

INTRODUCTION


Over the last few years the UK has faced an unprecedented
number of major civil emergencies - both natural and Ban Bade.
Bradford, Zeebrugge, Piper Alpha, King's Cross, Clapham
Junction, Lockerbie, Kegworth, Purley, Hillsborough have
become inextricably linked with events involving major loss of
life, injury and trauma. These events, together with other
problems such as the storms which hit Southern England in
October 1987, have tested in extreme conditions our ability to
respond to major disasters. The first~line response of the
emergency services has been widely praised but the scale of
these and other disasters has nonetheless focussed attention
on how effectively the present - largely decentralised -
response can meet the needs of major incidents and whether
anything could be done to improve it.

2.      During the course of 1987 and early 1988 many
individuals and organisations were considering the
implications of recent incidents. Seminars were held at
Bradford University and the Royal Military College of Science,
Cranfield, involving participants from a wide range of
disciplines and interests. The views and recommendations
arising from these were considered by the Borne Office together
with a large number of opinions expressed by individuals
anxious to register their views. Detailed submissions were
produced by many with an active professional interest in
emergency planning, and many were also received from
interested members of the public.
3.      The ideas and proposals put forward vara variad and
wide ranging. Soma auggastad only minor rafinamanta or
additions to axiating atructuras, while othara proposad a
radical ahift from the local rasponaa to a naw cantralisad
control ovar tha planning for, and rasponsa to, amargancias in
tha UK.


4.    it was claar that thara was a wida divaraity of opinion
not only about tha marits of axiating arrangamants but also
about tha naad, or methods, to improva tham. It was also
apparant that most of tha mora far-reaching suggastions had
not baan axplorad in datail with the emergency sarvices and
local authoritias who currantly hava tha oparational
responsibilitias for dealing with any disastar and whose views
and practical judgments would be essential. There were also
questions of finance and existing statutory responsibilities
which had not been addressed.


5.     Accordingly, in April 1988 the Home Secretary asked
officials to conduct a thorough review of the current
arrangements for dealing with civil emergencies. He asked
that all the suggestions and criticisms which had been
received should be explored fully and discussed formally with
fck* «mergency services and others, with a view to seeing what
practical improvements could be made to the existing systems.

CONDUCT OF THE REVIEW

6.     The review was carried out in two distinct phases: the
first was a wide consultative exercise, the second a senior
level seminar at the Civil Defence Collage.

Consultative Exercise
 7.     In June 1988 the Home Office produced a discussion
 document which sought to bring together many of these
 disparate strands (copias of th is vare placed in the L ib raries
 of both Houses). The document identified seven main areas for
 discussion. These covered:


              1.   the ro le o f c e n tra l government;

              2.   statutory requirements for civil contingency
                   planning;

              3.   national co-ordination;


              4.   public information/casualty bureaux;

              5.   the collation of technical and operational
                   information;

              6.   the role of voluntary bodies;

              7.   provision for aftercare.

8.     in each case the document set out the existing
procedures, identified the gaps which some contributors felt
existed, detailed the general thrust of the varied suggestions
made, and drew out some of the implications, it was made
clea^ that the suggestions discussed were without prejudice,
and were offered for open discussion to ensure a full debate
     ***• issues; the document did not c o n s titu te Government
p o lic y proposals.

9.     The discussion document was widely circulated.
Initially it was sent to the associations representing police,
fire and ambulance services, emergency planners, local
authorities, and voluntary bodies; central government
departments were also asked to consider the implication* for
them. This initial distribution involved some 130
organisations and associations. Additionally the document vas
sent to many others who had carried out substantial work on
the subject, or were involved in a less Immediate way (eg.
professional training colleges). The document vas freely
available and vas sent to anyone requesting a copy.

10.    I9hen the consultative exercise vas formally concluded
in August 1988 about 70 full responses had been received.
Officials analysed the responses, and the information obtained
served as a basis for the second phase of the review.

11.    In producing both the discussion document and papers
for the seminar officials took account not only of the
individual contributions made but of some larger issues. The
experience of all recent disasters vas taken into account as
far as possible. The position in other countries vas
considered to see vhether their experience could be of help,
or vhether their different systems, infrastructures, and,
indeed, the nature of risks faced, meant that their
arrangements for responding to an emergency vere necessarily
different. Particular attention vas paid to Belgian systems
in viev of their able handling of the Berald of Free
Enterprise disaster at Zeebrugge.
    *
Civil Emergencies Seminar

12.    The analysis of the responses to the discussion
document identified four main areas for thorough discussion.
These vere:
            1.   the collation of information and access to
                 expertise (Including tha rola of computer data
                 bases);

            2.   tha local authority dimension (involving
                 planning responsibilities and tha longar tana
                 naads of thosa affactad in a disastar);

            3.   casualty buraaux (both Banning and tachnical
                 cosununications issuas);

            4.   administrative structures nacassary to achiava
                 an effective fully co-ordinated response (both
                 cantrally and locally) to any incident.

13.    A aaminar vas hald at the Civil Defence College
Easingwold from 23-25 November 1988 to discuss these issues in
detail vith those with operational responsibilities. Nearly
80 individuals took part, representing - at a senior level -
the police, fire and ambulance services; EM Coastguard;
various local authority associations; emergency planners;
voluntary bodies and government departments.

CONCLUSIONS OF THE SEMINAR

14.    There vas a vide-ranging and open discussion about the
issues under consideration. While there vere naturally
differences of emphasis the broad conclusions of the seminar
vere as follow*:


       1.   the operational response to a disaster should
            remain at the local level; there vas no support
            for an interventionist "national disastar squad";
         2.   more could be done to encourage and develop co­
              ordination at local level between the various
              services and authorities involved, especially in
              t e n s of joint planning;


        3.    although the case for legislation imposing a duty
              on local authorities to plan for peacetime
              disasters was strongly argued by a number of the
              local authority representatives present, the
              prevailing view was that on balance there was no
              immediate need for such legislation and the
              general view was that there was room for
              improvements in co-ordinated local planning which
              should be explored fully before reaching a view on
              th® need for legislation;

        4.    in terms of central government co-ordination the
              "lead Department" concept remained valid although
              it was not well understood outside Government and
              should be more clearly explained;

        5.    there was considerable support for (and no dissent
              from) propositions that arrangements to give a
              flfltlgnal oversight of the progress of co-ordinated
              planning could lead to improvements in our
              response to emergencies.
    %
CONCLUSIONS OF THE REVIEW

15.   Taking account of all the views expressed at the
seminar, the ideas put forward in the course of the review,
    the experience of recent incidents, the Home Secretary hflg
reached the following conclusions.
16.    The basis of the response to particular disasters
should ramain at tha local level: it is here that axpertise
and knowledge exists, that tha bast information is available,
and it is at this laval that co-ordination between tha various
agendas or individuals involved can provida tha most
effective rasponsa. There had bean no support from the
practitioners for a 'national disaster squad*: it was felt
that a dedicated unit of this type would not assist in making
the existing response more affective and could in fact cause
problems in co-ordination and control. Such a squad would of
necessity be inactive for most of the time and in many
instances not be able to arrive on scene until the immediate
response was well underway. As the UK has emergency services
with considerable resources dispersed throughout the country,
able to call on colleagues for general or specialist
assistance, there is no operational need for an additional
body.

17.    Although some local authority representatives had
argued for a statutory duty to plan for peacetime emergencies,
the Home Secretary is not convinced of the need for it at
present. Local authorities are able to plan for peacetime
emergencies using their own resources under s.138 of the Local
Government Act 1972 (and the equivalent legislation for
Scotland and Northern Ireland) and there was no avidence to
suggest that local authorities were not generally already
undertaking a planning function (in former metropolitan
countries it is the boroughs/districts which have the
necessary s. 138 powers, and can either plan individually or
arrange for the joint Fire and Civil Defence Authority to
carry out such planning collectively as their agent). While
this position will be kept under review it is claar that much
can be done without legislation and this should be pursued as
far as possible as the immediate priority.
18.    A clear need has been identified for new arrangements
to give national oversight to the development of co-ordinated
emergency planning, and to addrass specific practical issues
raised by recent disasters. Additionally more needs to be
done to encourage and develop co-ordination between the
various services at the local level.

19.    Co-ordination of the central Government response can
most effectively be carried out by the lead department,
supported where necessary by arrangements for collective
discussion organised under Cabinet Office auspices in the
normal way. But the linkages between this machinery, the
various inspectorates and the operational emergency services
are not well understood and need to be more clearly explained.

20.    To meet these needs the Home Secretary has decided to
appoint a Civil Emergencies Adviser, to work closely with
senior representatives of all the groups which have
operational or administrative responsibilities in this field.
In addition there will be an enhanced role for the Civil
Defence College (to be renamed the Emergency Planning
College).

CIVIL EMERGENCIES ADVISER


21. ' The Civil Emergencies Adviser will be charged with the
oversight of the whole area and will be reporting directly to
the Home Secretary. Be will draw together the wide range of
administrative, operational and inspectorate interests
involved with major disasters, and encourage and promote co­
ordinated planning both locally and centrally. Be will be
likely to travel widely to assess the state of civil emergency
planning on the ground and to recommend changes where this
will lead to increased effectiveness of the response as a
whole. He will not have operational responsibilities in the
event of a major emergency, but would be closely involved in
scrutinising the lessons to be learnt and ensuring they are
disseminated and applied.


22.     He will work closely with senior representatives of
the emergency services, local authorities, voluntary bodies,
safety inspectorates, Government Departments and others
directly concerned, and will be assisted by a Secretariat
within the Home Office working in association with the Cabinet
Office. His task will be:


     To consider matters of current concern in the field of
     civil emergency planning in peacetime, with a view to
     helping those with operational responsibilities achieve
     the highest standards of co-ordination and compatibility
     between their contingency arrangements.

23.    The Adviser will not be empowered to override existing
statutory or other formal responsibilities, but will explore
relevant issues with all those concerned, to establish the
most effective way of resolving problems. His particular
concerns will be with:


     1.    the development and dissemination of good practice
    '      and information, in particular about local co­
            ordination;

     2.    ensuring that the lessons of recent and past
            experience are learnt and applied;

     3.    assistance with exercises and training;
      4.     liaison with all inspectorates concerned with
             public safety;

     5.      liaison across central Government Department*,
              local government, tha emergency services, tha
             voluntary bodiaa and others with a diract
              intarast;

     6.      devalopment of tachnical arrangement* in
             conjunction with usars and suppliers.

24.    A number of specific issues hava baan idantifiad in tha
coursa of tha raviav to vhich ha will ba asked to giva
priority. Thasa includa tha handling of casualty anguirias,
psychological damage to survivors and relatives, assistanca
for foraign disasters, and training and exercises. In a
numbar of casas work on particular issuas is alraady taking
placa and this will continua. Tha Advisar may wish to consult
tha bodias concamad and draw on tha rasults of thair work to
ansura that tha maximum banafit can ba drawn from currant
studias. In other casas ha may wish to convana groups of
axparts to assist him in tha examination of datailad
quastions.


Eggrgsngy.,.-Planning Cplltgf


25. * To support tha Advisar's work tha Civil Defanca Collage
at Easingvold vill ba givan a wider remit and will ba axpactad
to address quastions of peacetime emergency planning,
irrespective of any wartime connections. This vill be
reflected in a change of name to "Emergency Planning Collage".
Tha Collage vill particularly focus on intar-disciplinary
needs and vill vork in conjunction vith tha Police Staff
Collage at Bramshill and tha Fire Service Collage at Moreton-
in-Karsh. It vill act as a centre for studies, exercises and
seminars, and as a focus for tha development of an
understanding of tha vide issuas involvad. Further seminars,
such as tha ona held in tha course of tha review, vill ba
arranged to provide sanior personnel vith an opportunity to
focus on tha major issuas collactivaly. In soma raspacts tha
College vill act as a study facility for issuas idantifiad by
tha Civil Emergencies Advisar. Tha necessary skills and
expertise to develop this additional role for the Collaga vill
have to ba davalopad ovar a pariod of tima.

OVERALL STRUCTURE


26.    Undar tha proposed nav arrangements this country's
rasponsa to major emergencies vill continua to hava two main
alaments, aach vith distinct responsibilities. Thasa ara:

     Tha emergency services, local authorities and others with
     operational responsibilities at local level.

     The "lead" Government department, supported through
     Cabinet Office machinery vhere necessary.

Both vill be assisted and supported by the Civil Emergencies
Adviser.


27. * The emergency services and others at the scene of a
disaster are responsible for dealing vith the immediate
effects, deciding on the need for additional resources and
asking for them, and keeping relevant Government departments
and the public in the surrounding area informed about progress
in dealing vith the effects of the emergency and any measures
necessary for public safety.
28.    The "lead” Government department is, whenever possible,
identified in advance and is responsible for coordinating
both the necessary central contingency planning and the
implementation of the plan should a disaster occur. The
"lead" department is also responsible for keeping Parliament,
other departments and the general public fully informed on
measures for dealing vith the effects of the disaster. Any
problems vhich cannot be resolved under "lead" department
auspices are referred to the Cabinet Office. Should a
disaster occur for vhich no "lead" department has been
nominated the Prime Minister, as Chairman of the Cabinet, can
decide quickly vhich department should take up this role.

29.     The Cabinet Office is responsible for supporting the
Cabinet and Cabinet Committees in the conduct of Government
business. Arrangements for dealing vith civil emergencies
are, like any other business, organised vithin this framework.
Specifically the Cabinet Office provides the machinery vithin
vhich Ministers can collectively discuss and reach decisions
on issues that need to be settled, eg the priority for
allocation of resources or matters that have not been resolved
by the "lead" department.


30.    The Civil Emergencies Adviser vill review the
effectiveness of the co-ordinated local response. Be vill
consider any practical or inter-disciplinary problems raised
and arrange further study or research as necessary. Be vill
reviev the broad lessons to be learnt from incidents at home
and overseas and will help in the development and
dissemination of guidance on best practice in association vith
the Departments or services concerned.

                  Please ask for:




22nd June 1989.


Mr. P. Goodman,
News Editor,
Sheffield Newspapers Limited,
York Street,
Sheffield.


Dear Sir,

With reference to our telephone conversation today, I wish to object at the
misrepresentation of fact contained in the article written by Donna Saul
and Nigel Morris in your newspaper on Wednesday 21st June 1989. The errors
occurred in the following points

1     The BBC were asked by the South Yorkshire Metropolitan Ambulance
      Service during the week following the inquiry if they would assist us
      in order to eliminate interference of our radio system which we
      believed may be arising from their transmissions of the World Snooker
      Championships at the Crucible Theatre, Sheffield. The BBC agreed to
      switch off in turn each of their three transmitters. Following that
      exercise it was established that the Ambulance Service radio system
      had not been affected by the BBC transmissions.

2    The wording in the article suggests that two emergency channels were
      left on within the ambulance control which caused interference. That
      is not what I said, I clearly told the inquiry that we had identified
      that another Ambulance Service had left open a radio channel when
      they had released vehicles to assist SYMAS that had caused the
      interference. I understand that the wording in your article
      basically suggests facts but clearly indicates that the problem was
      associated to the South Yorkshire Ambulance Service. That is not the
      case and that was clearly emphasised to the inquiry.

3    The article also shows that I criticised the police radio system. I
      did not criticise the police radio system. I was asked to comment on
      talk through which is a method in which radio systems generally can
      be utilised. My comments related to experiences within the Ambulance
      Service and the complications which I believe from an Ambulance
      Service point of view talk through can create. I never gave an
      opinion about the operation of talk through from a police point of
      view, nor was I asked to do so. Had I been asked to comment I would
      have restricted my comment purely to the Ambulance Service as I am
      not responsible for any of the police operations.

4    I would ask you to clarify the large headline which indicates "999
      channels interfered with urgent messages". That was never said, nor
      did it happen and is clear misrepresentation as the 999 channel is a
      totally different channel than the one being utilised for the
      disaster.
                                                                                       CP65/32586
22nd June 1989.


Mr. P. Goodman.

I should now like to refer to the article with regard to Mr. Hopkins.

5    Mr. Hopkins did not say that the control room at the South Yorkshire
     Ambulance Service had misdirected ambulance crews, nor did he say
      that that had hampered the rescue. He did say that he heard
      ambulances being directed to the Leppings Lane end over his radio and
     he knew from our plan that the only access to the ground was through
      the entrance at the Penistone Road North side. As it turned out
      there were two incidents that SYMAS had to deal with, one outside the
     Leppings Lane entrance and the second one on the pitch behind the
     goal at the Leppings Lane end. The comments contained in your
     article with regard the control room are inaccurate and create
     professional embarrassment for this service as well as a
     misrepresentation of fact to the general public.

6    In the same section of your article concerning Mr* Hopkins1
     appearance before the inquiry it clearly states that Station Officer
     P. Eason went to the Liverpool end of the ground with a fireman
     colleague                    [.                  is a Qualified
     Ambulanceman employed by this service and he accompanied Mr. Eason.

These points appear to be the major concern of myself and my officers. I
would point out that we experience a very good rapport with the Sheffield
Star and the points raised in this letter are aimed merely at ensuring the
factual information concerning this service is passed on to the general
public.

I should be grateful for your assistance in ensuring that the matter is
speedily resolved in terms of submitting a factual report to the general
public contained in a prominent position as this has caused embarrassment
within the service and may cause embarrassment between this service and the
South Yorkshire Police.

If you require any further information, please do not hesitate to contact


Yours sincere




CHIEF METROPOLITAN AMBULANCE OFFICER

                           Stress Debriefing

    A course for Chief Ambulance Officers and their Senior Staff.


Date:    4th to 6th April, 1989,

Venue:    Percevall Hall, Appletreewick, North Yorkshire.

Cost:    ^.450   (inclusive of accomodation, meals and tuition.)

Course Directors:

                              , Director - Centre for Crisis Psychology

         Dr.Alma James, Senior Consultant -                    "    "

         <j                          I      Senter for JCriaapsykologi-,—



The aim of the course is to provide officers with a thorough
grounding in Post-Traumatic-Stress-Disorder, its effects within
the personal and occupational spheres, and Critical Incident
Stress Debriefing.

  "A CISD has several functions. Through the process people are
helped to ventilate their impressions and reactions, and at the
same time they have   a chance to learn that their reactions are
not unique or abnormal. The debriefing decreases tension, leads
to cognitive organisation and helps in mobilising resources witin
and outside the group."

6* INTRODUCTORY PHASE

This is very important ~ decreases likelihood of malfunction.

Personal   introductions

"I  am X ....  this is a proceedure I've used many  times                                                  before
following difficult,  stressful incidents,  such as ....                                                   It's a




                    Directors: M. Stewart M .A., CQSW., P. H odgkinson B.A., M .Phtl., A.F.B.Ps.5.
  proceedure  most people find* hel pfu l, allowing us the chance to
  •hare some of the thoughts and -feelings about the experience, and
  find out that they are shared by others and are very much normal,
  which helps prevent any long term problems".
■':                                                  ■
  Rules

 (i) No one has to say anything at all if they don't want, only to
 start  with their name and connection with the  incident.    "You
 are, however, requested to listen".

 (ii)  Everything   that is said is confidential,  to us and  group
 members  - no -management person will take anything that  is said
 outside, no notes will be made.

 (iii.) The       meeting is not -a critique,   nor is it    a   proceedural
 debriefing.

 (iv) We only talk for ourselves and our own reactions,            not those
 of others.

 (v) We may feel worse during tfte session,        but this is n o r m a l , as
 we are getting in touch with pain.

 (vi ) There will be no breaks, so have a pee now...

 (vij,,) People  do,  of course,  have the opportunity to  leave              -
 "please^ hiowever, leave quietly, but come back if possible".

 (ix) Give-brief outline of what will happen.

 (x)    "Any questions?"

 1 *. FACI PHASE

 "What   happerted?"  Each person briefly describes what  happened.
 How did they come upon the event?    What was the time sequence of
 events?    Foster brief factual cross-questions.

 AIM    - Clear     correct picture of facts of event is available         for
 all.

 El ItiQUBHI PHASE                                                             i

 Focus on decisions and thought processes.     "What were your first
 thoughts?"     "What did you do?"   "Why did „you decide  on  doing
 what you did?" (often related to protecting o t h ers ). "What were
 your  impressions of what was going on around you as events took
 their course?"    "What did you hear/smel1 /see?".

 Questions about thoughts very often lead into feeling answers.
D R E A C T I O N PHASE

This is o-ften the longest part o-f the debriefing.

NBs Allow feelings!

When   people   describe reasons behind decisions they     will  often
talk   about fear and helplessness     (loneliness),  self   reproach,
frustration    etc.     It is this that establishes similarities and,
normality.        Other     past    events    may    surface      (work
rela ted /personal) - facilitator must separate & give     suggestions
about working through of earlier reactions.

"How did the events make you react?"
"What was the worst of what happened for you?"
"Have  you ever ex peri en£fe<iy^nythi ng in your life before that has
made you so upset/frustrated/frightened?"
"How did you feel when X h a p p e n M ^ l ^ ® ^ , , ^

NB:  Facilitator  must allow eyeryone the chance to participate -
must  intervene  if  ground rules are   broken,    or  if there   is/
destructive  criticism.      Remember - you are modelling for them
how to deal with it with each' other/fami 1 ies/friends in the weeks
to come.

 If someone breaks down,  mobilize peer support - signal neighbour
 to put an arm round,  verbalize support etc.    If person leaves,
 co-leader to follow to encourage re-entry. ^

 Watch  for      who  seem to suffer  a  lot/are   silent/have  extreme
 symptoms.         Approach  individually at end, and offer individual
 support.

 Describe symptoms during event
                          on return home
                          the following day
                          now

 "What    unusual    things    are        you     experiencing      now?"
 EMQTIONAL/COGNITIVE/PHYSICAL.

 "What is different from usual?"
 "Was it difficult to return to work?"
 "Has this had an impact on you and your family?"
 "Do people   at home understand what you've been       through?"    (NB:
 lack of acknowledgement).

 One person describes symptoms - "Have you felt like him .... ?"

 REMEMBER  THE  THREE ELEMENTS OF PTSS  - REEXPERIENCE       PHENOMENA,
 AVOIDANCE/NUMBING, AND INCREASED AROUSAL.
E*.   teaching   phase


Identify  normality by reference to research/material       from   other
debriefings.

Tell  people  what   reactions  to expect.      Emphasise  "Normal
reactions to abnormal events".     Don't suggest that they HAVE to
react in such a way,   but that the following feelings are normal
(see attached).    Emphasise individuality as well.

§5. BiziNIRY PHASE

Sum up.
Hand round written material.
"Any. questions?"

"How can personnel help each •othfr?^
NBs   Acceptance  of  reactions   as<''ttor*mal ,   and   importance   of
understanding each other.

"When do you need further help?"
If symptoms don't decrease, ’s^fy, after h weeks,  or if symptoms
increase over time, or if they handicap work and/or family-life.

"Do we need a follow-up debrief?"
Whole or part group?    Is there an issue that needs to.be         dealt
with^in specific depth?

"What are'Tuture contact points?"
How to contact team/occupational health etc.

                            C-fci-'ffcRi/y               ________________________



A.   The person has experienced an event that is outside the range of
     usual human experience and that would be markedly distressing to
     almost anyone, e.g., serious threat to o n e ’s life or physical
     integrity; serious threat or harm to o n e ’s children, spouse, or
     other close relatives and friends; sudden destruction of o n e ’s
     home or community; or seeing another person who has recently been,
     or is being, seriously injured or killed as a result of an

     accident or physical violence.


B.   The traumatic event is persistently reexperienced in at least one

     of the following ways:


     (1)    recurrent and intrusive distressing recollections of the
            event (in young children, repetitive play in which themes or
            aspects of the trauma are expressed)
      (2)   recurrent distressing dreams of the event
      (3)   sudden acting or feeling as if the traumatic event were
            recurring (includes a sense of reliving the experiencej
            illusions, hallucinations, and dissociative [flashback]
            episodes, even those that occur upon awakening or when

            intoxicated)
      (4)   intense psychological distress at exposure to events that
            symbolize or resemble an aspect of the traumatic event,

            including anniversaries of the trauma
                     Directors: M. Stewart M .A., CQSW., P. Hodgkinson B.A., M .Phil., A.F.B.Ps.S.
                4




C.   Persistent avoidance of stimuli associated with the trauma or
     numbing of general responsiveness (not present before the trauma),
     as indicated by at least three of the following:


     (1)   efforts to avoid thoughts or feelings associated with the
           trauma
     (2)   efforts to avoid activities or situations that arouse
           recollections of the trauma
     (3)   inability to recall an important aspect of the trauma
           (psychogenic amnesia)
     (4)   markedly diminished interest in significant activities (in
           young children, loss of recently acquired developmental
           skills such as toilet training or language skills)
     (5)   feeling of detachment or estrangement from others
     (6)   restricted range of affect, e.g., unable to have loving
           feelings

     (7)   sense of foreshortened future, e.g., does not expect to have
           a career, marriage, or children, or a long life


D.   Persistent symptoms of increased arousal (not present before the
     trauma), as indicated by at least two of the following:


     (1)   difficulty falling or staying asleep
     (2)   irritability or outbursts of anger
     (3)   difficulty concentrating
     (4)   hypervigilance
     (5)   exaggerated startle response
     (6)   physiologic reactivity upon exposure to events that symbolize
           or resemble an aspect of the traumatic event (e.g., a woman
           who was raped in an elevator breaks out in a sweat when
           entering any elevator)


E.   Duration of the disturbance (symptoms in B,C, and D) of at least
     one month.




Specify delayed onset if the onset of symptoms was at least six months
after the trauma.
206 Daniel S. Weiss, Mardi J, Horowitz and Nancy Wilner


Table 1, Item stems and definitions o f the Stress Response Rating Scale

Intrusion items
  1. Hypervigilance - Excessively alert, overly scanning the surrounding environment,
      overly aroused in perceptual searching, tensely expectant.
  2. Startle reactions - Flinching after noises, unusual orienting reactions, blanching
      Or otherwise reacting to stimuli that usually do not warrant such responses.
  3. Illusions or misperceptions - A misappraisal o f a person, object, or scene as
       something or som eone else. (e.g. a bush is seen for a mom ent as a person;
      a person is misrecognized as someone else.)
  4. Intrusive thoughts or images when trying to sleep - Unwelcome and unbidden mental
      contents that may be difficult to dispel; include trains o f thought that begin
       volitionally but develop an out-of-control quality.
  5. Bad dreams - A ny dreams experienced as unpleasant, not just the classical nightmare
       with anxious awakenings.
  6. Hallucinations, pseudohallucinations - An emotional reaction to imagined stimuli,
      experienced as if it were real, regardless o f the person's belief in its reality.
       'F elt presences’ o f others as well as sensations o f smell, taste, touch, m ovem ent,
       sound and vision are included, along with out-of-body experiences.
  7. Intrusive im ages while aw ake - Unbidden sensations which occur in a non-volitional
       manner either in visual or other sensory systems. Awareness o f these images is
       unwanted and occurs suddenly.
  8. Intrusive thoughts dr feelings while awake - Unwilled entries o f simple ideas or
       trains o f thought and feeling taking unwilled directions.
  9. Reenactments - A ny behaviour that repeats any aspect o f the serious life-event,
       from minor tic-like movements and gestures to acting out in major movements and
       sequences, including retelling the event. Repeated enactments o f personal responses
       to the life-event, whether or not they actually occurred at the time o f the event.
 10. Rumination or preoccupation - Continuous conscious awareness about the event
       and associations to the event that go beyond ordinary thinking through. The
       key characteristic is a sense o f uncontrolled repetition.
 11. Difficulty in dispelling thoughts and feelings - Once a thought or feeling has
       come to mind, even if it was deliberate, awareness o f it cannot be stopped.
 12. Pangs o f emotion - A wave o f feeling that increases and decreases rather than
       remaining constant.
 13. Fears or sensations o f losing bodily control - Sensations o f urinating, vomiting,
       or defaecating without will, fear o f suffocating, fear o f being unable to control
        voluntary behaviour as well as somatic responses such as sweating, diarrhoea, tachycardia.
Denial avoidance item s
14. Inattention, daze - Staring off into space, failure to determine the significance
     o f stimuli, flatness o f response to stimuli.
15. M em ory failure - Inability to recall expectable details, sequences o f events,
     or specific events.
16. Loss o f train o f thought - Temporary or micromomentary lapses in continuation o f
     a communication, or report o f inability to concentrate on a train o f thought.
17. Numbness - Sense o f not having feelings, or being ‘benumbed’. (Note'. Either
     patient report or your inference is acceptable here.)
18. Sense o f unreality - Experiences o f depersonalization, derealization, or altered
     sense o f time and place.
19. Withdrawal - Feelings or action* indicating social isolation or experiences o f
     being isolated and detached.
20. Misdirection o f feelings - Displacement o f positive or negative feelings.
21. Excessive use o f alcohol or drugs - Avoidance o f implications o f the event by
     increased usage. Alcohol: excessive usage. Drugs: abuse o f prescription agents,
      as well as abuse o f other drugs, legal and illegal.
                                                                   S tr e s s R esp o n se R a tin g S c a le   207


Table 1. (cont.)
22. Inhibition o f thinking - Attempts to block thinking about the event. Success
     or awareness o f the attempt is not a consideration.
23. Unrealistic distortion o f meanings - Effects o f the event on day-to-day living
     are inaccurately appraised.
24. Excessive sleeping - Avoidance o f implications o f the event by increased sleeping
     as well as by simply staying in bed.
25. Avoidance o f reminders - Staying away from certain places, foods, or activities;
     avoiding photographs or other mementos.
26. Seeking o f distracting stim ulation or activity - Avoidance o f the implications o f
     the event by seeking excessive exposure to external stimuli or activities such as .
     television, loud music, fast driving, sexual activity, voracious reading, or
     other diversions.
General items
27. H yperactivity - Fidgeting, markedly increased pace o f activity, inability to slow
     down or stop sequences o f actions; periods o f frenzied activity.
28. R etarded pace o f actions - Psychomotor retardation: clear slowing, either
     continuous or episodic, o f thought or behaviour.
29. Tremors or tics - Tremors or tics, including tics about the eyes and mouth.
     (N ote: Basis o f tremor or tic as neurological or characterological is irrelevant.)
30. Clumsiness or carelessness - D ropping objects, bumping into furniture, actions
     that are more than awkward.
31. Autonomic hyperarousal - Sweating, palpitations, frequent urination, altered
     skin colour, altered pupil size, or other autonomic signs.
32. Troubled sleep - Inability to fall and stay asleep: bad feelings about or during
     sleep.
33. Restlessness or agitation - Report o f inner sensations o f agitation or action and
     behaviour which is restless or agitated.
34. E xcited stales - Thought and action is dominated by excessively high rate o f
     arousal, information processing, and expression. May include excessively high
     levels o f sexuality, creativity, productivity, exercise.
35. Self-hatred - Uncontrollable suicidal preoccupation or gestures, self-loathing.
     or hostility toward a part o f the b o d y .
36. Rage at others - Uncontrollable hostility and anger, even if the target is unclear.
37. Panic or disintegration - Periods oi" high pressure, confusion, chaos, anxiety,
     and purposelessness.
38. Sadness - Uncontrollable sadness or grief: floods o f despair, longing, pining.
      or hopelessness.
39. Guilt or shame - Out-of-control experience o f remorse, sense o f wrongdoing, or
      exposure o f personal evil or defectiveness.
40. Irritability or touchiness - Relations with peers, children, or strangers that
      are either inwardly irritating or outwardly abrupt, hostile, and bristling.



 topics discussed were fairly similar, differences both in the interviewer and the presenting complaint
 o f the subject led to some variation in the protocol o f the interview.
    The elapsed time between the serious life-event and the date o f the interview was uncontrolled for
 the two sets o f 16 patients, resulting in 32 o f the 48 initial evaluations showing a wide range o f
 variation on this variable.

 O th e r m e a su res
 After their interviews, subjects completed a battery o f self-report instruments. Among them were the
 Symptom Checklist-90 (SCL-90; Derogatis et at., 1973) and the Impact o f Event Scale (IES;
 Horowitz et at., 1979; Zilberg et a!.. 1982). The SCL-90 is scored for nine dimensions o f
 psychopathology such as anxiety, depression and somatization, as well as a total pathology score.
        — INTRODUCTION —




People and organisations react very differently to traumatic
 events, while most, in time, will cope with the emotional
  upheaval. Many will have great difficulty readjusting to
                 normal life after a crisis.
The Centre for Crisis Psychology exists to help people and
   organisations in this critical readjustment period. We
    encourage self evaluation and self awareness of the
psychological affects of traumatic experience. Emphasising
 and supporting the individual's natural resilience is often
crucial to the successful management of the psychological
             after affects of such an experience.

                                                                                          relationships or responsibilities o f central Government
                                                                                          Departments. The Department with the closest involve­
                                                                                          ment acts as “lead department” co-ordinating the central
                                                                                          G overnm ent response, organising the necessary executive
                                                                                          action and keeping Parliament and the public informed.
                                                                                          The lead department is supported by arrangements for
                                                                                          collective discussion should that prove necessary. The new
                                                                                          arrangements are complementary to existing arrange­
                                                                                          m ents and will provide as a new element, a central focus
                                                                                          for the shared views and experience o f the emergency
                                                                                          services, local authorities and voluntary bodies.

                                                                                          com prehensive statement about the conduct and outcom e
                                                                                          o f the review.

Dear Peter,

I write to confirm the content of my telephone conversation with you on
Monday 17th April 1989, in that I am now making a very strong request to
you and to other organisations to take what ever action is necessary to
have Chief Ambulance Officers given statutory powers to monitor andprovide
ambulance service advice and cover within sports stadiums which would
include football grounds. These statutory powers should not just be for
football matches but for any large gathering of people.

I should be grateful for any comments you would like to make.




A. Page,
CHIEF METROPOLITAN AMBULANCE OFFICER

21st April 1989.




Dear

I am writing to personally Thank You for the way in which you responded to
the incident at Hillsborough on Saturday 15th April 1989. I know that for
those of you who were on the ground and saw the trauma at first hand, it
will have created many bad memories which I am sure none of you will
forget. For those who were in the control ensuring that assistance and
back up was being supplied I know that the pressure and the content of the
messages which were being received both by telephone and over the radio
from the ground shocked everyone.

It has become more and more obvious over the last few days whilst having to
debrief everyone, just how professional you were and although there were
many fatalities, no one must blame themselves. These fatalities were not
created under any circumstances by any member of this service and I know
just how frustrated we all were as the death toll increased.  Unfortunately
that is what the Ambulance Service has to be ready to respond to at
anytime.

I can only express how proud I was at the way in which all of you performed
your duties and I would like to Thank You all for your efforts. If you
require any help or assistance over the next few weeks or months you must
not hesitate to contact any officer of this service. We must all do what
we can to assist one another to come to terms with and get over as many of
the memories as we can.

Once again, many thanks for the very professional way in which you
responded.

Your




A. Page,
CHIEF METROPOLITAN AMBULANCE OFFICER

                           DEPARTMENTAL MEMORANDUM



Ref:      AP/                                 Date:   20th April 1989

Fron:     Mr. A. Page                         To:     All SYMAS Personnel
          C.M.A.O.



MAJOR INCIDENT - HILLSBOROUGH FOOTBALL GROUND - 15TH APRIL 1989

I wish to place on record my gratitude for the way in which all
personnel, irrespective of rank or position dealt with the situation at
Hillsborough on Saturday. The South Yorkshire Metropolitan Ambulance
Service conducted themselves in a very professional and efficient manner,
in what can only be described as a devastating event.

I know everyone who was involved both at Hillsborough and within the
Control Unit suffered drastically from the effects of what happened and
what was seen. I can only say that this service performed its duties in
such a way and in very difficult circumstances both physically and
emotionally which brings credit to everyone of us. It is now up to each
one of us to help one another to come to terms with the trauma and build
the service up again to where it was before this sad event took place.
We must do all we can to assist anyone within the service who suffers in
any way from being involved with this disaster and if anyone requires any
form of support through physcology counselling etc they must not hesitate
to contact any officer from this service who will make the necessary
arrangements.

The matter is now a part of the judicial enquiry being conducted by Lord
Justice Taylor and this necessitates me debriefing everyone concerned
with the incident and hopefully this will be finalised over the next week
or so. Because of this enquiry, I must reiterate my instructionto all
personnel that no comment must be made to the press.   The matter has to
be treated by this service to be of an extremely confidential nature,
with me as the only service spokesman.

I know many people telephoned the service, many of whom were actually
asked to attend stations, others whose names were taken to be called upon
if they were required at a later stage of the incident. To those people,
I would also like to say Thank You as I can confirm that from a control
officer point of view in charge of the incident it was extremely
reassuring to know that more man power and help was available should the
incident be prolonged or in any way become more severe.

I can only express once again my sincere thanks and gratitude for the way
in which all of you responded and conducted yourselves, you are yet again
a total credit to SYMAS and myself.
                                                                    t


A. Page

21st April 1989.


Mr. V. Joliffe,
National Secretary,
The Association of Chief Ambulance Officers.



Dear Mr. Joliffe,

I am pleased to note the content of the Association of Chief Ambulance
Officers (Scotland) news release. I have to inform you that the South
Yorkshire Metropolitan Ambulance Service did in fact, through its own
initiative, and without payment place officers at Hillsborough prior to the
kick off.

I cannot add to this statement until the judicial enquiry has been
complete.

Dear Mr. Jukes,

I should be extremely grateful if you can pass on my sincere thanks and
gratitude to the members of your service both control and operations who
assisted us in dealing with the very distressing events which took place at
Hillsborough last Saturday.

I am sure that it has affected all of them and if there is anything at all
that this service can do to assist them in coming to terms with the trauma
that they experienced I should be grateful if you would let me know.

We do have stress counsellors available and if you or your staff think that
they would like to be involved with that, then you only have to ask.

From the Ambulance Service point of view it is clear that the response to
the incident was very good. Obviously I cannot go into too much detail at
this moment in time because of the judicial enquiry, but the assistance of
your personnel was a bonus which was greatly appreciated.

Yours si

Fire chief talks of delay
in call to Hillsborough



     EIGHT                                                                                 “SHEER MAYHEM” . . . Help for one of the fans caught in the chaos at Hillsborough




     LOST MINUTE
HEARTBROKEN firemen told today of their frantic
and courageous battle to save the lives of dozens of
fans in the Hillsborough disaster and claimed there
had been an important eight-minute delay by the po­
lice in calling out the Sheffield brigade.
  It was claimed for the first time that
the authorities in the stadium under es­
timated the scale of the disaster and the
desperate urgency to call in firemen
equipped with the skills and life saving
apparatus.

chief officer Alan Seaman said: “We did not

Press Release for the South Yorkshire County Fire Service


FIRE SERVICE RESPONSE TO THE HILLSBOROUGH DISASTER

The South Yorkshire County Fire Service today (Wednesday, 19th April,
1989) decided to put the record straight about its involvement in the
Hillsborough disaster.

Mr. Harry Wright, the South Yorkshire Chief Fire Officer, said:      "The
South Yorkshire Fire Service is mindful of Lord Justice Taylor's concern
about uninformed speculation about events at Hillsborough football ground
on   Saturday,   15th April, 1989.     Unfortunately an inaccurate and
misleading account of the Service's response to the Hillsborough disaster
is emerging in the press.

To avoid this, I can confirm that the initial call for assistance from
Hillsborough was received by the Fire Service Headquarters Control Room
at just before 3.14 p.m. The call informed the Service that there was a
major incident at Hillsborough football ground.    It also informed the
Service that there was a specific requirement for cutting gear. The
Service's standard attendance to such an incident - 6 (six) pumps, one
emergency tender and a control unit - was mobilised from County fire
stations at 3.16 p.m. Each pump appliance carries cutting gear and the
emergency tenders carry heavier duty cutting gear.

The first appliance reached Hillsborough at about 3.22 p.m. along with
two others, well within the call-out standard for the distance they had
to travel (2 to 3 miles). The officer in charge of the first unit on the
scene assessed the situation and immediately requested the attendance of
a further four pump appliances and stressed that it was the additional
resuscitation (oxygen) equipment they carried that was required.     All
pump appliances carry oxygen equipment.

The pumps required and an additional emergency tender were mobilised and
additional supplies of oxygen were released from Fire Service Stores.

At the ground the firefighters were faced with a large number of
seriously injured persons who required emergency first aid treatment.
Using their skills they undertook to assist other emergency services in
providing essential life saving functions.

The firefighters worked with and alongside the police, ambulance and St.
John Ambulance Brigade, ground stewards and officials and many hundreds
of spectators.

The South Yorkshire County Fire Service will be co-operating fully with
the inquiry into the Hillsborough disaster being headed by Lord Justice
Taylor.

The Service will be preparing a detailed statement of its involvement in
the tragedy for submission to the inquiry.

The Service will not publish its inquiry statement until Lord Justice
Taylor authorises that this can be done. The Service has no wish to pre­
empt the findings of the inquiry nor to fuel further speculation about
the Hillsborough disaster."

                                          THE CATHEDRAL SHEFFIELD SI 1HA Telephone 0742 753434

                              Hillsborough Disaster

I write to tell you that we are arranging a Memorial Service for all concerned
in the Hillsborough Disaster in Sheffield Cathedral, Church Street, on Sunday
April 23rd at 6.30 pm.      Would you please make this as widely known as possible.

Everyone will be welcome.

If official representatives intend to be present, it would be helpful for us
to know.   (Telephone 0742 753434)

Our thoughts and prayers are with you all at this tragic time.

            Further to our telephone conversation of Monday 18th April 1989 concerning
            the tragic events at Hillsborough. I confirm that should you find it
            necessary to transfer back to Merseyside a large number of patients and
            require assistance our Staff and Officers have offered their services
            whether on duty or off.

            This kind of response from the Merseyside Ambulance Staff reflects our
            appreciation of South Yorkshire Metropolitan Ambulance Service Staff for
            the professional manner in which a very distressing situation was dealt
            with.

            I understand many of your Staff have requested stress counselling following
            the incident and I wish them to know that their fellow professionals in
            Merseyside are thinking of them at this very sad time for your Service and
            our County.

            Kind regards.


            Yours sincerely
